  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4f210c4c-a108-4ab1-b4e0-03416b9ac478'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12443454750152938062"><i>Jesus' Son</i>, the first collection of stories by Denis Johnson, presents a unique, hallucinatory vision of contemporary American life unmatched in power and immediacy and marks a new level of achievement for this acclaimed writer. In their intensity of perception, their neon-lit evocation of a strange world brought uncomfortably close to our own, the stories in <i>Jesus' Son</i> of</span>
  <span id="freeText12443454750152938062" style="display:none"><i>Jesus' Son</i>, the first collection of stories by Denis Johnson, presents a unique, hallucinatory vision of contemporary American life unmatched in power and immediacy and marks a new level of achievement for this acclaimed writer. In their intensity of perception, their neon-lit evocation of a strange world brought uncomfortably close to our own, the stories in <i>Jesus' Son</i> offer a disturbing yet eerily beautiful portrayal of American loneliness and hope.<br /><br />Contains:<br />Car Crash While Hitchhiking<br />Two Men<br />Out on Bail<br />Dundun<br />Work<br />Emergency<br />Dirty Wedding<br />The Other Man<br />Happy Hour<br />Steady Hands at Seattle General<br />Beverly Home'</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/458003-you-wouldn-t-rely-on-the-unnamed-narrator">You wouldn&#39;t rely on the unnamed narrator of these stories to go to the drugstore for you, or take your car to the car wash, or babysit your little sister, but when he talks to you, you believe every word he says. Why would Denis Johnson make him such a reliable narrator when he&#39;s such a fuckup - even by his own admission - about everything else?</a>
</div>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I think the underlying theme of F.H's humanity is extremely compelling, in a way that most readers might not notice it at first glance. The underlying<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I think the underlying theme of F.H's humanity is extremely compelling, in a way that most readers might not notice it at first glance. The underlying tones of who this man used to be really resonate deeply, not just with addicts and the like.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer31096266" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4114138309965901907">I once fell in love with a man just because he recommended this book to me. He had a glass eye and fingernails with with half moons of crust lodged underneath, thick and dark as coffee grounds. He was living covertly and temporarily for about four years in one of those storage units out by the interstate, and I would sometimes go see him when I wanted to get high or feel better about my life. At some point he died when they blew up a bridge to build a dam, and he happened to be sleeping undernea</span>
  <span id="freeText4114138309965901907" style="display:none">I once fell in love with a man just because he recommended this book to me. He had a glass eye and fingernails with with half moons of crust lodged underneath, thick and dark as coffee grounds. He was living covertly and temporarily for about four years in one of those storage units out by the interstate, and I would sometimes go see him when I wanted to get high or feel better about my life. At some point he died when they blew up a bridge to build a dam, and he happened to be sleeping underneath it. Or maybe that was someone else. Maybe it was some kids from the high school found him sleeping under the bridge. It was late and they were drunk and had just lifted a stop sign off the road and, proud of it, they thought they might try to break it over his head. In any event, he died of some head trauma of the most religious sort, that much I know.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5865914493184504753"><em>"I could understand how a drowning man might suddenly feel a deep thirst being quenched."</em><br/><br/>Magnificent, concise writing. A calm sort of sad, and strangely relaxing. Stories about people living in the corners of society.<br/><br/>It’s like I’m sitting on the porch of a shack in the middle of nowhere, listening to the saddest old man I know tell me misremembered stories about how shitty he was when he was young.<br/><br/>It all feels extremely real, like lives that were lived.   <span id="freeText5865914493184504753" style="display:none"><em>"I could understand how a drowning man might suddenly feel a deep thirst being quenched."</em><br /><br />Magnificent, concise writing. A calm sort of sad, and strangely relaxing. Stories about people living in the corners of society.<br /><br />It’s like I’m sitting on the porch of a shack in the middle of nowhere, listening to the saddest old man I know tell me misremembered stories about how shitty he was when he was young.<br /><br />It all feels extremely real, like lives that were lived. The stories are connected, and all share a protagonist. It's an ethereal novella of sorts, each story like a chunk of truth torn from reality, with otherwise fiction filling in the cracks.</span>

  <span id="freeText15340253425313405923" style="display:none">In the psychedelic world everything is surreal… Everything is blurred…<br /><blockquote>The downpour raked the asphalt and gurgled in the ruts. I sensed everything before it happened.</blockquote><br />In the phantasmagoric surroundings even hopes and pursuits turn surreal…<br /><blockquote>And with each step my heart broke for the person I would never find, the person who’d love me. And then I would remember I had a wife at home who loved me, or later that my wife had left me and I was terrified, or again later that I had a beautiful alcoholic girlfriend who would make me happy forever.</blockquote><br />Environs are certainly psychedelic but <i>Lucy in the Sky with Diamonds</i> seems to be flying someplace else… So far away from here…<br /><blockquote>…one small orange flower that looked as if it had fallen down here from Andromeda, surrounded by a part of the world cast mainly in eleven hundred shades of brown, under a sky whose blueness seemed to get lost in its own distances.</blockquote><br />Day in, day out… Life goes on in a trashy whirlwind… No purpose… No meaning…</span>

<div id="review_3368319267" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3368319267" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36555254-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536187488p2/36555254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3368319267?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847086709" href="/book/show/15956637-jesus-son">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3368319267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4583356141899200548">Jesus walks here, among the degenerates, the drunks, the addicts, the living dead that populate the pages of this collection of very short, connected stories. You'll see him in beautiful moments, outstretched hands seeking redemption, the presence of an unlikely trinity of down and outers.<br/><br/>Yes, Jesus. Even though the title refers to lyrics in Lou Reed's song, "Heroin". Jesus and heroin are mixed here. A potent, jarring combination. <br/><br/>The same character who spends his sober evenings as a peeping tom</span>
  <span id="freeText4583356141899200548" style="display:none">Jesus walks here, among the degenerates, the drunks, the addicts, the living dead that populate the pages of this collection of very short, connected stories. You'll see him in beautiful moments, outstretched hands seeking redemption, the presence of an unlikely trinity of down and outers.<br /><br />Yes, Jesus. Even though the title refers to lyrics in Lou Reed's song, "Heroin". Jesus and heroin are mixed here. A potent, jarring combination. <br /><br />The same character who spends his sober evenings as a peeping tom and having twisted fantasies also spends his days working in the hospitals, touching the hopeless and downtrodden with a hand to the shoulder, a reassuring squeeze. The same couple who are drunken strangers in a bar (the woman newly married just a few days) have a connection that one could only describe as divine:<br /><br /><i>First I put my lips to her upper lip, then to the bottom of her pout, and then I kissed her fully, my mouth on her open mouth, and we met inside.<br /><br />It was there. It was. The long walk down the hall. The door opening. The beautiful stranger. The torn moon mended. Our fingers touching away the tears. It was there.</i><br /><br />Characters here are so close to death, it's not surprising they find Jesus close by. Jesus never had a problem with slumming. He's there on the subway, by the fire in the metal trash can, in the abortion clinic, at the scene of the car crash, in the ER. <br /><br />Denis Johnson is an exquisite, poetic writer. It makes sense that he was taught by Raymond Carver at the Iowa Writers' Workshop. Both writers accomplish much in few words, have a beautiful spareness that cuts to the quick. I would say I had a more emotional reaction to his final collection, <i>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</i>, but the power and truth contained in <i>Jesus' Son</i> just can't be denied.<br /><br /><i>It was raining. Gigantic ferns leaned over us. The forest drifted down a hill. I could hear a creek rushing down among the rocks. And you, you ridiculous people, you expect me to help you.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="4583356141899200548" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_89520808" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/89520808" />
    <a title="Nancy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/763271-nancy"><img alt="Nancy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435597527p2/763271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/89520808?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy" href="/user/show/763271-nancy">Nancy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nancy by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/1248986-evan">Evan</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=homelessness">homelessness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=addiction">addiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=library-books">library-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=shelf-inflicted">shelf-inflicted</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer89520808" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14329609310966294646"><b>Posted at <a href='http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2015/01/jesus-son.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shelf Inflicted</a> </b><br/><br/>I wasn’t sure if I would like this collection of loosely connected stories about a young guy who is addicted to drugs, sometimes homeless, sometimes employed, and occasionally steals. He’s not an especially likable character, but I enjoyed being a part of his thoughts, his views, and his haphazard journey through life. Maybe it's because I have empathy for addicts and others who live on the edge.<br/><br/>This powerful and gripping collection of stories was troubling, intense, an</span>
  <span id="freeText14329609310966294646" style="display:none"><b>Posted at <a href="http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2015/01/jesus-son.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Shelf Inflicted</a> </b><br /><br />I wasn’t sure if I would like this collection of loosely connected stories about a young guy who is addicted to drugs, sometimes homeless, sometimes employed, and occasionally steals. He’s not an especially likable character, but I enjoyed being a part of his thoughts, his views, and his haphazard journey through life. Maybe it's because I have empathy for addicts and others who live on the edge.<br /><br />This powerful and gripping collection of stories was troubling, intense, and humane. I was overwhelmed by its beautiful language and poignant passages.<br /><br />One of my favorite stories in this collection is <i>Dirty Wedding</i>, a sad and unsettling little story about abortion, loneliness, heroin addiction, and death. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“The wheels screamed, and all I saw suddenly was everybody’s big ugly shoes. The sound stopped. We passed solitary, wrenching scenes. Through the neighborhoods and past the platforms, I felt the cancelled life dreaming after me. Yes, a ghost. A vestige. Something remaining.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br /><i>Beverly Home</i> was sad, a little humorous, and very hopeful. The young narrator finds a part-time job in a nursing home, spies on a Mennonite couple in their bedroom, and begins a life of sobriety. <br /> <br /><i>
  <blockquote>“All these weirdos, and me getting a little better every day right in the midst of them. I had never known, never even imagined for a heartbeat, that there might be a place for people like us.”</blockquote>
</i><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14329609310966294646" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_910164633" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/910164633" />
    <a title="J. Kent Messum" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23165988-j-kent-messum"><img alt="J. Kent Messum" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442882127p2/23165988.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/910164633?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J. Kent Messum" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J. Kent Messum" href="/user/show/23165988-j-kent-messum">J. Kent Messum</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23165988-j-kent-messum?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23165988-j-kent-messum?shelf=dangerous-writing">dangerous-writing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23165988-j-kent-messum?shelf=eye-opening">eye-opening</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23165988-j-kent-messum?shelf=gritty">gritty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23165988-j-kent-messum?shelf=great-read">great-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23165988-j-kent-messum?shelf=hard-hitting">hard-hitting</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23165988-j-kent-messum?shelf=masterful-stuff">masterful-stuff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23165988-j-kent-messum?shelf=must-read">must-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23165988-j-kent-messum?shelf=masterpiece">masterpiece</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23165988-j-kent-messum?shelf=what-writers-read">what-writers-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer910164633" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5392335574513304573">*I'm heartbroken to hear of Denis Johnson's recent passing. The man was a personal hero and great literary influence of mine. I'm floating this review of his quintessential masterpiece as a tip of the hat to an exceptional author who brought us some of the finest prose. <br/><br/>Without a doubt, this is one of my favorite works of all time. Denis Johnson is a major influence of mine, and Jesus' Son had a profound effect on me. <br/><br/>This was the book that showed me how far you could stretch your prose and stil</span>
  <span id="freeText5392335574513304573" style="display:none">*I'm heartbroken to hear of Denis Johnson's recent passing. The man was a personal hero and great literary influence of mine. I'm floating this review of his quintessential masterpiece as a tip of the hat to an exceptional author who brought us some of the finest prose. <br /><br />Without a doubt, this is one of my favorite works of all time. Denis Johnson is a major influence of mine, and Jesus' Son had a profound effect on me. <br /><br />This was the book that showed me how far you could stretch your prose and still have it sound dynamite. A drug-addled mix of loosely interconnected stories, reading it is like navigating a string of dreams, both blissful and bad. The spectrum of themes is considerably wide, and the narrative draws you into worlds where you can feel as uncomfortable and out of place as the characters themselves. This is a book that actually makes you feel 'high' in some spots. The shifting line between chemical-fueled fantasy and uneasy reality isn't just blurred, it's burned down to almost nothing. <br /><br />Jesus' Son features everyday kind of people who have slipped down notch or two into the gutter and lost their grip on normality. Much of the time they have nothing to do, nothing to be, and little to live for. It's bleak and beautiful at the same time, a trip through the entangled emotions of folks living simple lives complicated by poor choices. <br /><br />Everything from love and loss to happiness and sheer horror is covered in this book. One moment the writing is slick as oil, the next it is jagged as broken glass. It’s downright chaotic in places where the mental states of the storytellers are in question. You know the main characters in this book are all unreliable narrators, but you still believe every word they say, because the stories Johnson tells are just that convincing. He's a master writer, balancing poetic passages with crisp, visual prose. This book is nothing short of a masterpiece.<br /><br />If you haven't read it yet, read is ASAP. <br /><br />*This book was one of my selections for my '5 Books That Made Me A Better Writer' piece. See which others I picked:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://jkentmessum.com/the-5-books-that-made-me-a-better-writer/">http://jkentmessum.com/the-5-books-th...</a><br /><br />On a side note.... if you have a chance to get the audiobook version, definitely do so. Actor Will Patton narrates the stories, and does an incredible job of bringing Johnson's prose to perfection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5392335574513304573" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer642753623" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer48922797803853614"><br/><b> I stayed in the library, crushed breathless by the smoldering power of all those words—many of them unfathomable.</b><br/><br/>Sometimes I judge and consequently love a book based upon the following points:<br/><br/>- A single, beautiful line I longed to read or hear in the words of some person other than me.<br/><br/>- A completely related character.<br/><br/>- A completely unrelated character.<br/><br/>- The way it makes me laugh.<br/><br/>- The way it makes me cry.<br/><br/>- The way it makes me feel extremely good about the life I’m leading.<br/><br/>- The way it makes me </span>
  <span id="freeText48922797803853614" style="display:none"><br /><b> I stayed in the library, crushed breathless by the smoldering power of all those words—many of them unfathomable.</b><br /><br />Sometimes I judge and consequently love a book based upon the following points:<br /><br />- A single, beautiful line I longed to read or hear in the words of some person other than me.<br /><br />- A completely related character.<br /><br />- A completely unrelated character.<br /><br />- The way it makes me laugh.<br /><br />- The way it makes me cry.<br /><br />- The way it makes me feel extremely good about the life I’m leading.<br /><br />- The way it makes me feel <i>rich</i>. (metaphor intended)<br /><br />- The way it makes me feel <i>poor</i>. (----ditto-----)<br /><br />- When a life is described without much fuss and the death is described without much glory.<br /><br />- When love is described without much purity and hate is described with a remarkable honesty.<br /><br />- When a few pages are enough to savor the taste of several slices of fucked up lives.<br /><br />- When few pages are actually not enough. <br /><br />- When a writer writes as if it’s the easiest and the most difficult thing to do at the same time.<br /><br />- When it’s so easy to love a book.<br /><br /><i>Jesus’ Son</i> met most of the aforementioned criteria and deserved a much better review but I hope you all got my <i>point</i> that I really enjoyed this book. A few hours reading, 11 really short stories basking in the glow of their respective singularities, some connections were made, some were left in isolation but all in all, a book worthy of my time and literary love.<br /><br /><b>
  <i> No more pretending for him! He was completely and openly a mess. Meanwhile the rest of us go on trying to fool each other.</i>
</b><br /></span>

<div id="review_2549375802" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2549375802" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2549375802?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780413772428" href="/book/show/9897.Jesus_Son">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=america-canada">america-canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2549375802" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer507258747231640803"><br/>"I'd been staying at the holiday Inn with my girl-<br/>friend, honestly the most beautiful woman I'd<br/>ever known, for three days under a phoney name,<br/>shooting heroin. We made love in the bed, ate<br/>steaks at the restaurant, shot up in the john,<br/>puked, cried, accused one another, begged one<br/>another, forgave, promised, and carried one an-<br/>other to heaven."<br/><br/><br/>Read a couple of Johnson's novels in the past and thought they were good but not great. These short-stories however were the real deal, and most of them total</span>
  <span id="freeText507258747231640803" style="display:none"><br />"I'd been staying at the holiday Inn with my girl-<br />friend, honestly the most beautiful woman I'd<br />ever known, for three days under a phoney name,<br />shooting heroin. We made love in the bed, ate<br />steaks at the restaurant, shot up in the john,<br />puked, cried, accused one another, begged one<br />another, forgave, promised, and carried one an-<br />other to heaven."<br /><br /><br />Read a couple of Johnson's novels in the past and thought they were good but not great. These short-stories however were the real deal, and most of them totally blew me away. I wouldn't be exaggerating to say that Jesus' Son, in regards to contemporary American short fiction, is up there with the best of Carver, Salinger, Yates and Cheever, to name a few. The biggest difference here though is junkies. <br /><br />These highly additive tales are as masterly controlled as their muzzy-headed characters are chaotic.<br />Johnson's world in governed by addiction, malevolence, faith and uncertainty. It is a place where attempts at salvation remain radically provisional, and where a teetering narrative architecture uncannily expresses both Christlike and pathological traits of mind. The narrative voices within come from down low, but like an assemblance of lostprophets, there is an undercurrent of religiousness about them. If the first story about a hitchhiking addict and a car crash was anything to go by, then I knew I'd likely end up with these burned into my memory; and I was right. <br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="507258747231640803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_602575123" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/602575123" />
    <a title="ΕιζΝιnΕ" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4483727-n"><img alt="ΕιζΝιnΕ" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597836400p2/4483727.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ΕιζΝιnΕ" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ΕιζΝιnΕ" href="/user/show/4483727-n">ΕιζΝιnΕ</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4483727-n?shelf=favorites-lit-historical-fiction">favorites-lit-historical-fiction</a>,

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer602575123" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16947662913491828142"><u>
  <b>Junk-sick, Broke and Completely Alone, in a Land of Bad Intentions</b>
</u><br/><br/>I've gone through three copies of ‘Jesus’ Son’, reading it like a prayer-book, though it’s nothing of the kind. There's a sadness living in every sentence, and it doesn't really have any suggestions for better living, beyond a painfully obvious cautionary tale. <i>Heroin=bad.</i> <br/><br/>The cautionary tale, however, is one interpreted by the reader. It's the best kind of dangerous, like a manual for poetic self-immolation, on burning yourse</span>
  <span id="freeText16947662913491828142" style="display:none"><u>
  <b>Junk-sick, Broke and Completely Alone, in a Land of Bad Intentions</b>
</u><br><br>I've gone through three copies of ‘Jesus’ Son’, reading it like a prayer-book, though it’s nothing of the kind. There's a sadness living in every sentence, and it doesn't really have any suggestions for better living, beyond a painfully obvious cautionary tale. <i>Heroin=bad.</i> <br><br>The cautionary tale, however, is one interpreted by the reader. It's the best kind of dangerous, like a manual for poetic self-immolation, on burning yourself down to the foundation-stones and finding one beautiful thing in the ashes. Even if it's something cheap and common, it assumes a spiritual density simply by escaping the flames, and a malleability that allows it to be shaped and worked into something vaguely redemptive. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448547191i/17157644.png" width="400" height="217" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>The power of Denis Johnson's prose is inestimable, with sentences &amp; imagery that will orbit the readers consciousness for decades. It's that kind of book: one that moves into your head and makes itself comfortable. His writing has the hard-eyed toughness of Chandler, but with a kind of unabashed sensitivity that Chandler would never expose... imagine Dashiell Hammett writing poetry, and imagine yourself liking it. It's Bogart in drag, or a concert pianist with the broken hands of a boxer. <br><br><blockquote>“And therefore I looked down into the great pity of a person’s life on this earth. I don’t mean that we all end up dead, that’s not the great pity. I mean that he couldn’t tell me what he was dreaming, and I couldn’t tell him what was real.”</blockquote> <br>See? That sort of thing. In case you're not familiar with The Velvet Underground, the title comes from one of Lou Reed's lines in 'Heroin'. <br><pre>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448545568i/17157423.gif" width="374" height="160" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</pre><br>Denis Johnson’s skills as a short story writer, poet, and novelist all coalesced with ‘Jesus’ Son’, to produce a sad, funny and frightening little masterpiece. It’s not really even a novella; more of a collection of short stories that add up to something related to a novella. Loosely inspired by Johnson's own experiences, the main character – Fuckhead - introduces himself with a story of hitch-hiking in the rain. He’s feeling burnt out and edgy after taking mystery pills from a travelling salesman... never a good idea, but his name <i>is</i> Fuckhead. He doesn’t do anything the right way. When a family is nice enough to stop and let a drenched stranger into their car, he knows that it's going to end in tragedy, but he gets in anyway...'They said they'd take me all the way.'<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448545568i/17157424._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="225" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Fuckhead's wanderings through the USA of the 1970's are always told from the anamorphic perspectives of a compromised mind, pleasantly poisoned with all manner of chemical. Junkies finding love in motel rooms. Drug-dealers half-heartedly murdering each other in a Midwestern farmhouse. A hospital clerk and orderly way too high for anything that comes through the Emergency Room doors. Rehab. Johnson presents skewed characters and situations that seem to operate according to the inscrutable mechanisms of dream and nightmare. Like Dundun, for instance, about whom I can say little. The narrator can say a few words, though, because narrators do that shit. It's their thing: <br><br><blockquote>“Will you believe me when I tell you there was kindness in his heart? His left hand didn't know what his right hand was doing. It was only that certain important connections had been burned through. If I opened up your head and ran a hot soldering iron around in your brain, I might turn you into someone like that.”</blockquote><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448547191i/17157645._SX540_.png" width="400" height="168" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><i>Yeah, that's Dundun. That's also perfect casting.<br><br>Alison Maclean's adaptation of 'Jesus' Son' is one of the best novel-to-film translations I've seen, along with 'No Country For Old Men' and the big screen incarnations of the aforementioned Chandler and Hammett. The performances are thoroughly fucking brilliant, but Michael Shannon's portrayal of Dundun was unforgettable.</i><br><br>Other writers could work a decade, write a thousand pages, and not create a fraction of the intensity and emotional dissonance that Johnson scribbles into being. And his characters... they crawl off the paper and bury themselves in the soft grey folds of your brain, like memories that you've bought and paid for by living them. <i>Jesus, that sounds absolutely fucking horrid</i>... but it’s actually a good thing. You can trust me... I'm not a doctor OR a politician<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448545455i/17157408._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="266" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><blockquote>“Talk into my bullet hole. Tell me I'm fine.” <br>― Denis Johnson, Jesus' Son</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="16947662913491828142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText14818214602213392419" style="display:none">I kind of sort of liked this woozy teensy bouquet of junkie memories but it was just too oh what’s the word even though the very sky above me was heavy with the five stars sluiced over this book by all previous readers in all the seven realms of readerdom. <br /><br />I got a mean and unworthy thought – that you could take sentences from almost anywhere in any of these stories and put them next to other randomly selected sentences and they would make as much sense, so I took something from page 20, 40, 80, 100 and 120 and proved it as follows:<br /><br /><i>In the room behind her the man we’d brought stood like a bad sculpture, posing unnaturally, with his shoulders wilting, as if he couldn’t lug his gigantic hands any further. There are large vague profound statements every so often. Reading this one felt like I was a first responder continually shouting in the author’s face “What is your name? What have you taken? Do you live here? Is there anyone I can call?”<br /></span>

<div id="review_718340156" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/718340156" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/236411-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291695844p2/236411.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/718340156?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/236411-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer718340156" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16058174140127824427">Denis Johnson took the fringe sensibilities of The Beats, added his own raw poetic touches, nicked a line from Lou Reed for the title, and ended up with an intensely unsettling collection of stories that prefigured to a T the drug classic <i>Trainspotting</i>. You may wonder at first if the unnamed narrator of these accounts could really be such an uncaring cad. Well, as a bottom line, maybe so. But the thoughts of murder, the thieving, and the ultra-callous disregard for fellow man were in large part </span>
  <span id="freeText16058174140127824427" style="display:none">Denis Johnson took the fringe sensibilities of The Beats, added his own raw poetic touches, nicked a line from Lou Reed for the title, and ended up with an intensely unsettling collection of stories that prefigured to a T the drug classic <i>Trainspotting</i>. You may wonder at first if the unnamed narrator of these accounts could really be such an uncaring cad. Well, as a bottom line, maybe so. But the thoughts of murder, the thieving, and the ultra-callous disregard for fellow man were in large part a function of the skag, the booze, and the stolen pharmaceuticals. This does not excuse the insensitivity and messed up behavior so much as it explains the eeriness, fragmentation, and reflexive anger. But amidst the distortions, bits of clarity stand out. And in contrast to the alienation in dull shades of gray, a kindness of any tint will catch the eye. <br /><br />Has it ever been proposed as an exercise in creative writing to imagine being (if not actually being) high as a kite? What am I saying? Of course it has. And abstract, luminous words have come from such otherworldly mindsets, I’m sure. In fact, I would argue that this short book set in the psychedelic 70’s is a prime case in point. It figures, with Johnson also being a poet, that the writing would be taut and expressive. He captures the sentiments of the down-and-out so well, too. For example, he describes how the “tears of false fellowship dripped on the bar” and how a gunshot victim should be happy he’s getting “Haldol pumped by the quart.” The drug scene is the backdrop, but the fallout is the real focus.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>” I'd been staying at the Holiday Inn with my with my girlfriend, honestly the most beautiful woman I'd ever known, for three days under a phony name, shooting heroin. We made love in the bed, ate steaks in the restaurant, shot up in the john, puked, cried, accused one another, begged of one another, forgave, promised, and carried one another to heaven.”</i>
</blockquote><br />My favorite parts were when little hints of what it means to be human snuck into the stories. It didn’t always put the narrator in a favorable light, but usually pointed to something redeemable within him. He appreciated co-worker and co-addict Georgie for telling a friend of a friend who was seriously AWOL that he’d help get him to Canada. Georgie also wanted to save the baby rabbits of the mother he ran over with his car. Another story described the narrator’s best day ever, one where he and a friend made $28 each from honest labors stripping copper wire out of the friend’s abandoned house and then celebrating with their favorite bartender pouring them double shots but charging them only for singles.<br /><br />The last story was set at Beverly Home, a place for senile, disabled, and disfigured adults where the narrator was given a part-time job as the newsletter writer and a point of human contact for those rarely touched. He was off drugs and sober at this point, and regaining his health. His straight and narrow path was still a little skewed, though, when he discovered a Mennonite woman who happened to time her shower every day as he passed by her place after work. As immoral as his actions were, though, there was a sense that he’d turned the corner <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I was going to say he turned the corner to find an odd looking guy with a long beard but no mustache about to aim a shotgun his way, but I’d be taking too much literary license saying that. It never happened. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />A book like this is an eye-opener. It’s a hard look at addiction, it’s an artistic peek at altered perceptions, and it’s a clever way to highlight humanity when set against the stuporous default settings. Oh, and since it matters, the writing was really good, too. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16058174140127824427" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_537527228" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/537527228" />
    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/537527228?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer537527228" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13990976155299262606"><i>“All these weirdos, and me getting a little better every day right in the midst of them. I had never known, never even imagined for a heartbeat, that there might be a place for people like us.”</i> <br/>― Denis Johnson, 'Jesus' Son'<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1444195721i/16467451._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='303' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>Sometimes while reading this I thought I was reading Burroughs (just not so dark), other times J.G. Ballard (just not so cold), sometimes even Palahniuk (but with more of a poet's heart). It was madness, a fever dream, tied together with beauty. It was fragments of insanity s</span>
  <span id="freeText13990976155299262606" style="display:none"><i>“All these weirdos, and me getting a little better every day right in the midst of them. I had never known, never even imagined for a heartbeat, that there might be a place for people like us.”</i> <br>― Denis Johnson, 'Jesus' Son'<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1444195721i/16467451._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="303" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>Sometimes while reading this I thought I was reading Burroughs (just not so dark), other times J.G. Ballard (just not so cold), sometimes even Palahniuk (but with more of a poet's heart). It was madness, a fever dream, tied together with beauty. It was fragments of insanity stitched together with the stars. And sometimes the night of this novel was so dark, I couldn't see the stars, and the blood all looked black. <br><br>I didn't personally like it as much as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12991188.Train_Dreams" title="Train Dreams by Denis Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">Train Dreams</a>, but that was just personal preference. I can see how some readers would absolutely adore it. It felt like a painting of blood or a beautiful photograph of a corpse. You are both attracted to and repelled by the art and the vision. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13990976155299262606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_4836804" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4836804" />
    <a title="Jonathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/294816-jonathan"><img alt="Jonathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521054724p2/294816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4836804?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan" href="/user/show/294816-jonathan">Jonathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">sigh...</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4836804" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17098106261726094962">Look, I don't know how else to put this. I recognize what Johnson's accomplished here, I acknowledge that he has a gift for phrase-level shine, and I concede that these semi-linked stories evince a remarkably coherent and vividly-depicted worldview that I might call "hopelessly optimistic," or maybe "tending to carry on when there's clearly no good reason to do so," or else, more succinctly, "Conradian" . . . but, I'm sorry, what I couldn't help but think/feel, wading through one after another o</span>
  <span id="freeText17098106261726094962" style="display:none">Look, I don't know how else to put this. I recognize what Johnson's accomplished here, I acknowledge that he has a gift for phrase-level shine, and I concede that these semi-linked stories evince a remarkably coherent and vividly-depicted worldview that I might call "hopelessly optimistic," or maybe "tending to carry on when there's clearly no good reason to do so," or else, more succinctly, "Conradian" . . . but, I'm sorry, what I couldn't help but think/feel, wading through one after another of these stories was, Yeah, I <i>know</i>: <br />I understand that the human elevator has no bottom floor, that a man can keep on falling til he's ready to pull the emergency brake and haul himself out onto some sublevel way below the bright clean fenestrated floors traversed by shiny people too stupid and lucky (so far) to know how shaky their footing is, how frightened they ought to be, and that the fallen subterraneans tend never to make it all the way back up to the light, but that this doesn't prevent them (us) from continuing to wriggle around through murky sub-basement muck, and that this perverse expression of the irrepressible will to move and breathe, to <i>go on</i>, no matter how shitty the environs or rancid the air, is what makes the condition of being human so simultaneously stupid and beautiful – <br />sure, I get this, but it's something I feel like I'd already got, i.e., I didn't need Denis Johnson to tell me, and if I <i>did</i> need to be informed that this is the way things are, I'm not at all convinced this book would have done it for me. Sorry.<br />Hope this doesn't get me kicked out of the writing program; I gather most of my fellows here sort of revere the man. As I say: he can clearly write. Let the angry response-posts commence.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17098106261726094962" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_17803611" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17803611" />
    <a title="Brent Legault" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/395634-brent-legault"><img alt="Brent Legault" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194719779p2/395634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/17803611?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brent Legault" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brent Legault" href="/user/show/395634-brent-legault">Brent Legault</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17803611" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5058720368635376717">This book ruined my reading bone for a long time. I wanted every story I read, every story by every other author, to be just like the stories in <i>Jesus' Son</i>. But of course they weren't and aren't and they stand alone in my mind, even now. Perhaps it's the whiskey talking, but I'd go so far as to call this little book one of the greatest of my generation. Not that such superlatives carry any weight anymore. I just can't get over this book. It was my first true love. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer304586151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10085543698254806507"><br/> To find Rick Bass's words of praise in the opening pages, speaking about this great 50,000-volt kick thrill of a book, I knew that this would be just the thing to cure my reading inertia. I'd followed a Carson McCullers novel like a dream into the rabbit hole, shrunk and dreamed until This One woke me like a cruel Queen. Consider me awake.<br/><br/>Not unlike characters from the early works of McCarthy, the faces that come in and out of focus in Denis Johnson's fictional world are victims of their own mi</span>
  <span id="freeText10085543698254806507" style="display:none"><br /> To find Rick Bass's words of praise in the opening pages, speaking about this great 50,000-volt kick thrill of a book, I knew that this would be just the thing to cure my reading inertia. I'd followed a Carson McCullers novel like a dream into the rabbit hole, shrunk and dreamed until This One woke me like a cruel Queen. Consider me awake.<br /><br />Not unlike characters from the early works of McCarthy, the faces that come in and out of focus in Denis Johnson's fictional world are victims of their own misfortune, unravelling fast, doing sad and cruel things and all the while saying &amp; thinking beautiful and stupid things. The central character / Narrator wanders stoned, drunk, high &amp; wretched into the most sublime heart-territory. When he's not prophetic he's outlandishly funny. <br /><br />The book is necessarily short at 133 pages, any longer and I might consider its beauty gratuitous. <br /><br />Lastly, I can't help but feel that the author sneaks in messages to his readers:<br /><br />"It was raining. Gigantic ferns leaned over us. The forest drifted down a hill. I could hear a creek rushing down among rocks. And you, you ridiculous people, you expect me to help you.<br /><br />*<br /><br />"Talk into my bullet hole. Tell me I'm fine".<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10085543698254806507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1020614910" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17703441782457944876">Is there a way of writing the right stories about the right people,telling everything neatly from start to finish. An Uppercase letter starting the story and a full stop waiting at the end. A boy meeting girl on the first page and walking away with her in the last one.<br/><br/>Or are stories like these...snapshots of nightmares which some would call hell, but is home to some. Where beautiful sentences strike you out of the blue, so beautiful that you read them again and again, flashes of lightning in a d</span>
  <span id="freeText17703441782457944876" style="display:none">Is there a way of writing the right stories about the right people,telling everything neatly from start to finish. An Uppercase letter starting the story and a full stop waiting at the end. A boy meeting girl on the first page and walking away with her in the last one.<br /><br />Or are stories like these...snapshots of nightmares which some would call hell, but is home to some. Where beautiful sentences strike you out of the blue, so beautiful that you read them again and again, flashes of lightning in a desolate sky.<br />A sky that won't shed tears for us anymore.<br />And if it did,<br /><i>I knew every raindrop by its name.</i><br /><br />Raindrops that cannot purify our soul. Our soul that is a ghost beyond redemption running hopeless to find love. Love that was never ours in first place.<br /><br /><i>With each step my heart broke for the person I would never find, the person who'd love me.</i><br /><br />So, what if you are loved.<br /><br /><b><br />And the night, oh the night when the wind<br />full of outer space gnaws at our faces; that wished for,<br />gentle, deceptive one waiting painfully for the lonely heart - <br />she'd stay on for anyone. Is she easier on lovers?<br />Ah,but they only use each other to hide what awaits them. <br /><br />-Rilke</b><br /><br /></span>

  <span id="freeText13538569560866803433" style="display:none">Find all of my reviews at: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/">http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/</a><br><br><a href="https://lunapic.com" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1573483005ra/28434765.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>And when it comes to fiction a bigly chunk of “my” people are drug addicts . . . . <br><br><a href="https://lunapic.com" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1573483005ra/28434766.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><i>Jesus’ Son</i> has been on my TBR for an age due to the fact that it is considered a modern classic and has appeared on list after list of must reads that I can’t ever stop myself from looking at, despite having 11,000,000,000,000 books already waiting for me to get to them. I can’t guarantee everyone will love this one – due to the aforementioned drug addict narrator along with a supporting cast of the same ilk – not to mention the fact that a lot of these shorts are real Debbie Downers. Buuuuuuuuuuut, the writing is pretty brilliant and my library copy literally fit in the palm of my hand (it is a miracle I did not lose it – I did, however, receive a past due notice because it’s so small I forgot I stuck it in my car console for safekeeping), so it’s not like it’s going to take much of your time. <br><br>Go read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/910164633?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">J. Kent Messum’s review</a> review for more details. He’s a writer by trade so he actually uses words rather than pictures to get his point across and he also talks about the audio, which is apparently read by the best reader of all time . . . . <br><br><a href="https://lunapic.com" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1573483005ra/28434767.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="13538569560866803433" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1815215467" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1815215467" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1815215467?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people with shitty nicknames</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1815215467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18058129944885755382"><i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i> in the boonies is what <i>Jesus' Son</i> feels like. It's an interconnected series of short stories starring the very down and very out in rural Iowa as they stagger through young adulthood. Its protagonist's name is Fuckhead, so there you go.<br/><br/>There's this great confused quality that's familiar to me from my own experimental days, which were much less dire (not at all dire) but, like, in one story they're all having a sendoff party for a friend who's going to jail, and midway throu</span>
  <span id="freeText18058129944885755382" style="display:none"><i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i> in the boonies is what <i>Jesus' Son</i> feels like. It's an interconnected series of short stories starring the very down and very out in rural Iowa as they stagger through young adulthood. Its protagonist's name is Fuckhead, so there you go.<br /><br />There's this great confused quality that's familiar to me from my own experimental days, which were much less dire (not at all dire) but, like, in one story they're all having a sendoff party for a friend who's going to jail, and midway through Fuckhead realizes that he has this all wrong, it's actually a welcome home party for the friend who's just gotten out of jail, so the entire story changes on a dime: "Oh shit, wait, that's not what's happening." I remember that sort of thing! Except it was more along the lines of whether we'd eaten all the gummy bears yet or not, so the stakes were a little lower.<br /><br />I like this better than <i>Last Exit</i>. I like both, but <i>Jesus' Son</i> avoids <i>Last Exit's</i> desperate shock tactics; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/863871701" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">my problem</a> with that book was that every story basically ended, like, "and then everyone got raped," and it felt a little obvious. Plus I like that "Jesus' Son" is from the Velvet Underground song <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=qTgDgYPnToo" title="Live!" rel="nofollow noopener">"Heroin."</a><br /><br />The thing with Fuckhead is that he has no self-esteem at all. He feels no shame when he peeps in some lady's window, because he has no shame left. He's made it down to raw animal level. I feel catharsis when I read characters like this, if they're well-done, as he is. I've dipped a toe or two into low self-esteem, at moments in my life. I don't think about it real often, because those were bummer moments. It's nice to understand that others know what it's like way down there where you can't see in front of your face. You don't read stuff like this too much. Cormac McCarthy's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1137392631" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Child of God,</a> maybe - Joan Didion's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1811568304" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Play It As It Lays.</a> But you don't see it often, because it's dark down there. Paradoxically, so, this book feels like a little light.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18058129944885755382" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_9163214" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9163214" />
    <a title="John Wiswell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/147818-john-wiswell"><img alt="John Wiswell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442531848p2/147818.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9163214?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Wiswell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Wiswell" href="/user/show/147818-john-wiswell">John Wiswell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Literary readers who've read all the good books</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9163214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16521033147031519155">I wouldn’t dislike this book so much if professors and literati hadn’t rubbed it in my face so much. Don't get me wrong - it wasn't entertaining, enlightening, intellectually arousing, and it didn't harbor any interesting characters or compelling scenes despite dealing with drugs, physical handicaps and multiple deaths. The narrator was far too pretentious with far too little beautiful writing or insight to pull it off. I was mostly bored or depressed, and occasionally outraged and how poorly wr</span>
  <span id="freeText16521033147031519155" style="display:none">I wouldn’t dislike this book so much if professors and literati hadn’t rubbed it in my face so much. Don't get me wrong - it wasn't entertaining, enlightening, intellectually arousing, and it didn't harbor any interesting characters or compelling scenes despite dealing with drugs, physical handicaps and multiple deaths. The narrator was far too pretentious with far too little beautiful writing or insight to pull it off. I was mostly bored or depressed, and occasionally outraged and how poorly written sections were, and even handed it to other students just to see if I’d gone insane – only to find they were equally unimpressed. But when you get someone like Yale’s Harold Bloom fondling it like a new-age Bible, I tend to lose it. This is a collection of short stories just tragically hip enough, just sad and bleak enough, just pretentious enough to be cuddled by literary-types. When Johnson isn’t failing on sentence-by-sentence level, he utterly fails to deliver story, compelling characters or satisfactory conclusions. Oh no. That’s below literary fiction, or drug fiction, or whatever this was supposed to be. Instead he strings together a few events, doing his best to steer things to negative conclusions, providing a little optimism and saccharine literary flourishes along the way. You can tell the depth of his fiction from the quote before the stories start, where Johnson reveals he didn’t get the title from the Bible or any of the slews of philosophical prose it spawned, but from “Heroin,” a Velvet Underground song. There is so little even vaguely allegorical or referential to a messiah, holiness, religion, or even a substantive search for greater meaning is a part of this collection that it becomes paintfully obvious Jesus was a sales ploy for the book and you have to make up a reason for the title to fit, or you're dumb. I’m not a Christian. I’m a reader and a thinking person, and both identities were insulted as I tried to delude myself into believing this had the deeper levels of an O’Connor or Kesey book. It didn't.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16521033147031519155" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1461697763" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1461697763" />
    <a title="Erika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23743588-erika"><img alt="Erika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1439597923p2/23743588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1461697763?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erika" href="/user/show/23743588-erika">Erika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23743588-erika?shelf=books-read-in-2015">books-read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1461697763" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9705470502854888074">This collection of stories is really short, but don’t let that fool you. Each word releases so much power that an hour spent reading <i>Jesus’ Son</i> carries the same impact as two hours of reading another book. Johnson throws off images like dazzling pieces of shrapnel, and for me, those gorgeous passages are what held the book together more than any actual plot.<br/>The narrator is a junkie with a life so chaotic that you always have the feeling anything could happen at any second. He might see an angel,</span>
  <span id="freeText9705470502854888074" style="display:none">This collection of stories is really short, but don’t let that fool you. Each word releases so much power that an hour spent reading <i>Jesus’ Son</i> carries the same impact as two hours of reading another book. Johnson throws off images like dazzling pieces of shrapnel, and for me, those gorgeous passages are what held the book together more than any actual plot.<br />The narrator is a junkie with a life so chaotic that you always have the feeling anything could happen at any second. He might see an angel, or he might see a man with a knife buried in his eye. Both occur and both are completely plausible. <br />All that said, I don’t believe <i>Jesus’ Son</i> is 100 percent deserving of its accolades. It’s a beautifully written portrait of particular people living in a particular time and place, but that’s about the extent of what I got from it. I was left wanting more…I don’t know…more to think about maybe. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9705470502854888074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28967523-claire-fuller?shelf=country-usa">country-usa</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2939356236" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15289467629582254717">God I loved this. A perfect collection of short stories. Fuckhead is in his early twenties and he's a drug addict and alcoholic. And no, a series of stories about drug-fuelled craziness narrated by this kind of man, wouldn't normally interest me, either. But the free-wheeling mind-altered narratives are so fresh and scary, and sometimes even funny. Don't be put off by the subject matter, just read it.</span>

          </span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/184928-r?shelf=1974-2002">1974-2002</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/184928-r?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5125056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer184316964611695625">The novelization of the Mountain Goats album <i>We Shall All Be Healed</i>. Or <i>We Shall All Be Healed</i> is actually <i>Jesus' Son: The Musical!</i>. Either way.<br/><br/>_____<br/>Note: <br/><br/>In "The Art of Reading Denis Johnson" (Poets and Writers, Nov/Dec 2013, pgs. 23-27), the guest columnist suggests that the line <i>I knew every raindrop by its name</i> is symptomatic of a "want of Wordsworthian affinity for the natural world, or a groping after a kind of Buddhist cohesion with the cosmos..." <br/><br/>I call bullshit on this Boston University</span>
  <span id="freeText184316964611695625" style="display:none">The novelization of the Mountain Goats album <i>We Shall All Be Healed</i>. Or <i>We Shall All Be Healed</i> is actually <i>Jesus' Son: The Musical!</i>. Either way.<br /><br />_____<br />Note: <br /><br />In "The Art of Reading Denis Johnson" (Poets and Writers, Nov/Dec 2013, pgs. 23-27), the guest columnist suggests that the line <i>I knew every raindrop by its name</i> is symptomatic of a "want of Wordsworthian affinity for the natural world, or a groping after a kind of Buddhist cohesion with the cosmos..." <br /><br />I call bullshit on this Boston University fellow's twee reading of the line. <br /><br />My take, if I'm allowed one, is that the line isn't some Adam's grand glorious reach towards the sparkling fingertip of a past Master, but rather the tossed-off remark of somebody (the narrator, Fuckhead) so storm-tossed and exhausted that, yeah, at this point in their journey, after so many hours standing in the rain, they have had enough. <i>Enough</i>. It's sarcasm. Like, for example, if you were stuck at an airport for half a day (or more in these airport times) you'd say, "Man, I should have my mail delivered here." So, if you're stuck in the downpour drunk and high and coming down and going up and veering sideways, yeah, you, too, would know every raindrop by its name. You're acquainted. You've become familiar. <br /><br />I wouldn't feel the need to vent, but the article's author takes gleeful pains to paint <i>Jesus' Son</i>'s main readership as "mid-twenties, white and male...revere <i>On the Road</i> and third-raters such as Charles Bukowski and William S. Burroughs...want to be fiction writers but have never read Henry James". <br /><br />But despite the snarky attitude, "The Art of Reading Denis Johnson" is worth tracking down if you see an old pile of Poets and Writers magazines, or are spinning at the microfiche machine wondering what to call up on-screen with that beautiful antique, as the article's author makes some helpful parallels between Johnson's novel and the works of Hemingway and Flannery O'Connor. <br /><br />Oh, and you'll learn more about good, clear writing from Bukowski than James. You will. You just <i>will</i>. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="184316964611695625" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
